201507	991	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	President George.	96	9
201507	992	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	993	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	Overall, I support a qualitative state- dependent approach to ceasing or phasing out reinvestments so we preserve optionality and managing decisions about the funds rate and the balance sheet.	96	9
201507	994	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	To convey the likely timing of the start of balance sheet normalization, I would be open to having the minutes convey views, to having the Chair speak to this at future press conferences, or to considering whether the SEP could be a possible communications vehicle.	96	9
201507	995	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	The table reporting the appropriate timing of policy firming in figure 2 could be replaced with a table reporting participants’ judgments about the appropriate time at which to begin to cease or phase out reinvestments once liftoff occurs.	96	9
201507	996	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	In terms of the policy itself, I would prefer not to distinguish between Treasury securities and MBS.	96	9
201507	997	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	In the longer run, I view a Treasury securities-only portfolio as most appropriate.	96	9
201507	998	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	For that reason, I would not want to further concentrate our holdings of MBS.	96	9
201507	999	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	And in light of uncertainty about how the market will respond to any adjustments in our reinvestment policy, I support gradually phasing out reinvestments over time.	96	9
201507	1000	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	After we announce the beginning of balance sheet normalization, I continue to support the approach in the normalization principles that reductions in our securities holdings will proceed in a gradual and predictable manner.	96	9
201507	1001	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	If possible, I would prefer to have adjustments to the pace of the phasing out of reinvestments being perceived as predictable rather than as subject to deliberations on a meeting-by-meeting basis.	96	9
201507	1002	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	The approach during the taper of asset purchases, where the process was put essentially on autopilot, barring any major surprise in the data, is relevant here.	96	9
201507	1003	2015-07-29	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you.	96	9

201507	1005	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	First Vice President Holcomb.	96	9
201507	1006	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1007	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	I share perspectives that several others have expressed already today.	96	9
201507	1008	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	As others have said, because the Committee has communicated that the timing of the cessation or phaseout of reinvestments will depend on the economic and financial conditions and the evolution of the economy, a pure calendar-based approach might be seen as a departure from that commitment.	96	9
201507	1009	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	That said, once we begin reducing the size of the balance sheet, we also should be consistent with the statement that we will do so in a gradual and predictable manner.	96	9
201507	1010	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	I take this second commitment to mean that once we actually begin reducing the balance sheet, we intend to rely on changes in the path of the federal funds rate rather than in the pace of balance sheet reductions for marginal adjustments to the amount of policy accommodation provided.	96	9
201507	1011	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	In qualitative terms, then, balance sheet normalization should not begin until we are reasonably confident that we won’t have to reverse course in order to avoid jeopardizing progress toward full employment and price stability—that is, until we are reasonably confident that subsequent policy adjustments will be achievable through manipulation of the federal funds rate.	96	9
201507	1012	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	The Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas staff suggests a comfortable point would be 75 to 100 basis points of leeway.	96	9
201507	1013	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Once the funds rate target is 75 to 100 basis points, or perhaps 100 to 125 basis points, it would seem reasonably unlikely that the zero bound would become a binding constraint on policy.	96	9
201507	1014	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	In sum, I would support a specified funds rate threshold for beginning balance normalization rather than calendar-based guidance or guidance based on a set of economic criteria that might be complicated or confusing.	96	9
201507	1015	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	On the second question—and, as a rookie, I guess I can answer the second question—we do make a distinction between the treatment of Treasuries and MBS.	96	9
201507	1016	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	The Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas view, as you may have heard in the past, is that the Federal Reserve should not play an ongoing role in providing support to the housing sector.	96	9
201507	1017	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	If housing finance remains difficult for certain groups of potential buyers, it is not the result of temporary liquidity problems but due to deliberate, nonmonetary regulatory and policy decisions.	96	9
201507	1018	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Nor do we see a rationale for continuing purchases of MBS as a countercyclical measure.	96	9
201507	1019	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	By the time the process of balance sheet normalization commences, we are likely to be at or very near full employment, with price stability in view.	96	9
201507	1020	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Accordingly, I favor as rapid an end to MBS reinvestments as is possible without disruption to housing finance.	96	9
201507	1021	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Ending reinvestments completely as soon as balance sheet normalization begins would be ideal, with MBS holdings being allowed to run off until they reach de minimis levels.	96	9
201507	1022	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	I would lean toward shrinking the balance sheet by a steady, predictable amount each month initially, consistent with our normalization pledge.	96	9
201507	1023	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Looking at exhibit 4, a reduction of something like $20 billion to $25 billion per month appears realistic.	96	9
201507	1024	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	We would see this as made up of sharp cutbacks on MBS reinvestments supplemented with enough of a scale-back of our Treasury securities investments to reach that level.	96	9
201507	1025	2015-07-29	meeting	HOLCOMB	Thank you.	96	9
201507	1026	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9
201507	1027	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Bullard.	96	9
201507	1028	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1029	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I have a view that picks up on themes that have been mentioned during the morning, but some aspects of what I will say are different from almost everybody.	96	9
201507	1030	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	My argument has four parts.	96	9
201507	1031	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	First, I think the balance sheet adjustment has proven to be a potent monetary policy tool, so I will talk about that.	96	9
201507	1032	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is a little bit in answer to Governor Fischer.	96	9
201507	1033	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Second, I think we should admit that it’s a potent tool when we are thinking about returning the balance sheet to a more normal size.	96	9
201507	1034	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Third, “potent tool” naturally means it needs to be managed carefully.	96	9
201507	1035	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think that means the adjustment should be state dependent.	96	9
201507	1036	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	A lot of people are saying it should be state dependent here this morning.	96	9
201507	1037	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Fourth— and this hasn’t been emphasized as much—we have an existing policy expectation, which is that we will cease reinvestment six months after liftoff.	96	9
201507	1038	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	My conclusion is:  We should choose a policy that is consistent with the existing expectation, but which is state dependent.	96	9
201507	1039	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Let me elaborate on these points.	96	9
201507	1040	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Regarding the balance sheet adjustment as a potent tool, surprise moves with respect to balance sheet policy have had large consequences, and the taper tantrum episode in the summer of 2013 is one important case for this Committee to consider.	96	9
201507	1041	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Strictly speaking, the tantrum itself only involved changes in expectations of balance sheet policy, not any actual change made by the Committee either at the June 2013 meeting or at the September 2013 meeting.	96	9
201507	1042	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Nevertheless, there were large ramifications, both in the United States and globally, on the order of 100 basis points on longer-term interest rates.	96	9
201507	1043	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is quite a big effect, indeed.	96	9
201507	1044	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think the staff’s models are likely understating potential effects on markets and on the economy that could come from changes in balance sheet policy.	96	9
201507	1045	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Also, the international evidence on balance sheet policy suggests important effects coming from the Bank of Japan’s most recent efforts and, certainly, the ECB’s most recent efforts.	96	9
201507	1046	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	My first point, then, is that this is a potent tool.	96	9
201507	1047	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	And I think we should be clearer that this is the case as we discuss this, because there is a lot of schizophrenia around the table.	96	9
201507	1048	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Some people think, “Oh, these are really small effects.”	96	9
201507	1049	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Well, if you think they’re small effects, then don’t worry about it.	96	9
201507	1050	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It doesn’t really matter when you normalize.	96	9
201507	1051	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	If you think they are big effects, as Governor Fischer was saying, then you have to be a lot more careful about it.	96	9
201507	1052	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Second, I interpret the staff simulations to suggest that if we manage the balance sheet down in a way that does not differ materially from current market expectations, then the effects will be minor.	96	9
201507	1053	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It is not that everything you do with a balance sheet causes big effects.	96	9
201507	1054	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It is only if you deviate from the current expectation, which is what the taper tantrum was.	96	9
201507	1055	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	We should expect small effects as long as we do something pretty consistent with existing expectations.	96	9
201507	1056	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	There is the potential for the effects to be large, and large effects would interfere with our policy rate normalization.	96	9
201507	1057	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Third, because the balance sheet policy is, in principle, quite potent, it needs to be managed carefully.	96	9
201507	1058	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	As many of you know, I have long advocated managing the balance sheet in response to economic conditions.	96	9
201507	1059	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	That means I am going to be in favor of a state-dependent approach.	96	9
201507	1060	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do not recommend going to numerical values.	96	9
201507	1061	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	That doesn’t sound like it’s getting too much support today.	96	9
201507	1062	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	A qualitative state-dependent approach, as advocated by President Dudley and others, would be what I’m advocating.	96	9
201507	1063	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	And fourth, as Governor Fischer talked about, we already have an existing balance sheet policy expectation built into financial markets, which is that we will end reinvestment six months after liftoff.	96	9
201507	1064	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	In my view, this policy is inappropriate.	96	9
201507	1065	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It’s too calendar based, and it does not smooth the balance sheet decline appropriately.	96	9
201507	1066	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	At the same time, I interpret the Committee’s sentiment to be that we do not wish balance sheet policy to interfere with the policy rate normalization process.	96	9
201507	1067	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	And I am sympathetic with President Lacker’s comment that you should be able to do this in such a way that you choose the optimal rate path, for an announced balance sheet policy, and that the implications for inflation and unemployment are exactly the same as they would otherwise be.	96	9
201507	1068	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think that is how we want to think about it:  We want to have primacy for our policy rate normalization process, and, therefore, we want to push balance sheet policy into the background, which is fine.	96	9
201507	1069	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	And we do not want some type of tantrum, moving longer-term rates either higher or lower than they would otherwise be in response to unexpected changes in the Committee’s balance sheet policy stemming from an announcement that we might make that this would be forthcoming.	96	9
201507	1070	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Such an outcome would interfere with the intended effects of the Committee’s main policy normalization, which would be taking place through interest rate increases.	96	9
201507	1071	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	So, what’s the bottom line?	96	9
201507	1072	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The task for the staff, as I see it, is to devise a smooth state- dependent strategy for this Committee to use to manage the balance sheet down to a lower level.	96	9
201507	1073	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	A simple version would be for the Committee to state that, provided the economy continues to make progress toward our goals, the Committee’s intention is to smoothly reduce the size of the balance sheet by a fixed amount—let’s say, $30 billion per month—at the appropriate time, but that the step size may be reduced if negative economic shocks occur and may be increased if positive economic shocks occur.	96	9
201507	1074	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This would presumably be close to the current market expectation and, therefore, would be unlikely to cause significant reaction in markets.	96	9
201507	1075	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Given the Committee’s past choices, the decision to change the pace of the manage-down would not be likely.	96	9
201507	1076	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	But it would be an important option if a major shock were to be encountered.	96	9
201507	1077	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	And surely if a major shock were encountered, probably the first thing we’d do is end reinvestment.	96	9
201507	1078	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	So I think you have to think about the ability to change the pace of “manage- down,” and I know several others around the table have said similar things.	96	9
201507	1079	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	As I see it, the “manage-down” would allow to MBS run off.	96	9
201507	1080	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The smoothing would be achieved by appropriately choosing Treasury securities purchases, sales, and reinvestment to achieve the given target, given the maturities on that particular month.	96	9
201507	1081	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is just one possible approach, but it gives us one example to look at.	96	9
201507	1082	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	In summary, the balance sheet is a potent tool.	96	9
201507	1083	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It should be managed smoothly and in a state-dependent way.	96	9
201507	1084	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	One goal is to stay close to current market expectations concerning reinvestment, so that interest rate normalization is not derailed.	96	9
201507	1085	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	A simple way to achieve these goals is to name a pace of reduction in the balance sheet itself but to retain the option to change that pace of balance sheet reduction in response to economic conditions.	96	9
201507	1086	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1087	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9
201507	1088	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	96	9
201507	1089	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1090	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I found this morning’s conversation regarding the thinking about this issue of halting or phasing out reinvestments very useful and interesting.	96	9
201507	1091	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought the discussion that once we reduce the balance sheet in size, there are big costs to reversing that reduction should really bear materially on our thinking about our policy in this space.	96	9
201507	1092	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One way I am thinking about it is that reducing the balance sheet, because we are reducing the amount of accommodation that would be in place when we hit the zero lower bound, is not that different from raising the effective lower bound itself.	96	9
201507	1093	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we hit the zero lower bound with a $4 trillion balance sheet, we have a certain level of accommodation in place.	96	9
201507	1094	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we hit the zero lower bound with only a $3 trillion balance sheet, we have considerably less accommodation in place.	96	9
201507	1095	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Another way to think about that is, we should be comfortable lowering the balance sheet when we are comfortable with the idea of raising the effective lower bound.	96	9
201507	1096	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, a couple of us—President Rosengren and I—have talked about our concerns about hitting the zero lower bound even in the longer run.	96	9
201507	1097	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think that makes you think about what our long-run goals for the balance sheet should be.	96	9
201507	1098	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we are sort of in this autopilot mode of thinking, “Boy, we should just get the size of the balance sheet back to 2005.	96	9
201507	1099	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That would be a good thing, because 2005 was a really good time.”	96	9
201507	1100	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the other hand, 2005 was only three years before 2008.	96	9
201507	1101	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	And two before 2007.	96	9
201507	1102	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	[Laughter]	96	9
201507	1103	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Yes.	96	9
201507	1104	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Governor Tarullo.	96	9
201507	1105	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That was helpful.	96	9
201507	1106	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should be thinking more systematically and deliberately about what we want our long- run balance sheet to look like.	96	9
201507	1107	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we are really averse in the longer term to the idea of ever increasing the size of the balance sheet because of the institutional or political risks that others have mentioned, I think that consideration really should shape our thinking about what that long- run balance sheet should be.	96	9
201507	1108	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I think it should also be shaping our thinking about when to initiate the phaseout of reinvestments.	96	9
201507	1109	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will say a couple of words about that in a minute.	96	9
201507	1110	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The other point—and this has come up a little bit, but I’ll try to emphasize it a little more—is that the amount of accommodation associated with our balance sheet—the size and duration of our holdings—is not shaped by the rate of change of the size of our holdings.	96	9
201507	1111	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So when we think about reinvestments, I think it’s important to be doing that in terms of how they are affecting the size and composition of the balance sheet, not in terms of reinvestments per se.	96	9
201507	1112	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This probably matters the most in terms of mortgage-backed securities.	96	9
201507	1113	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Because they are redemptions, we are delivering pro-cyclical variation in accommodation.	96	9
201507	1114	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	You can imagine households and businesses becoming more pessimistic about the prospects for the economy, of interest rates falling, and of mortgage refinancing increasing.	96	9
201507	1115	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As the mortgages underlying those securities in our portfolio are prepaid, our balance sheet shrinks at a faster-than-expected rate.	96	9
201507	1116	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So we are removing accommodation exactly when we need it.	96	9
201507	1117	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we want to be careful in thinking about reinvestment not in terms of the flow of reinvestments, but rather in terms of how it is affecting the stock of holdings on the balance sheet.	96	9
201507	1118	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those are my high-level points.	96	9
201507	1119	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should be thinking more about what our long- term goal is in terms of the balance sheet, especially in light of what I hear, which is a feeling of concern regarding, or aversion to, initiating a new asset purchase program.	96	9
201507	1120	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And we need to carefully reformulate our reinvestment policy to serve our balance sheet policy.	96	9
201507	1121	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That long-term goal, as I hinted at earlier, really should not just be shaped by a desire to get back to normal times.	96	9
201507	1122	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should be taking into account the fact that we a very bad outcome occurred that we didn’t anticipate, and we should be keeping in mind that, as we go forward, we might want tools to deal with that kind of outcome.	96	9
201507	1123	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn to the questions—though, having said that, I suspect I won’t be answering the questions because I am not a rookie.	96	9
201507	1124	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I like the idea of a state-based approach.	96	9

201507	1126	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	A couple of people—I remember President Mester doing this, and I think First Vice President Holcomb also mentioned this—suggested that we tie the initiation of phaseouts or reinvestments to the path of the federal funds rate.	96	9
201507	1127	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So we could announce very soon that the phasing out of reinvestments will begin when the target range of the federal funds rate hits X percent.	96	9

201507	1129	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Here I would disagree with some of the numbers I heard earlier.	96	9

201507	1131	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Remember what I said at the very beginning:  When would you be comfortable raising the effective lower bound for interest rates?	96	9
201507	1132	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Maybe you’re comfortable with that prospect at 1 percent.	96	9
201507	1133	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I myself would not be.	96	9
201507	1134	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would think we would want a higher target range for the federal funds rate before we go through the relatively irreversible process of shrinking the size of the balance sheet.	96	9
201507	1135	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think the staff could be helpful on this by giving us some simulations that would show us a probability of hitting the zero lower bound if we are at 1 or at 2 percent.	96	9
201507	1136	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think that it could provide an analytical framework for the Committee to think through those matters.	96	9
201507	1137	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	At that point, once we start, I would just announce that the evolution of phaseouts of reinvestments would depend on the evolution of the economy.	96	9
201507	1138	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, if you go with my 2 percent target range for the federal funds rate, that actually gives the Committee quite a bit of time to work on the details of the phaseout strategy.	96	9
201507	1139	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Before you do that, though, I think a key part of that work is to settle as much as possible what the long- term vision for the balance sheet is.	96	9
201507	1140	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Rosengren mentioned that the Conference of Presidents’ Financial Stability Committee went through a tabletop exercise in which two of us saw that actually selling assets might be a useful way to respond to a particular financial stability risk.	96	9
201507	1141	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Well, if you want that as part of your vision, it should be affecting the size of your balance sheet.	96	9
201507	1142	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you are averse to ever buying assets again, that should be affecting the long-term size of your balance sheet.	96	9
201507	1143	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	All of these things should be going into thinking about where you want to be headed.	96	9
201507	1144	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Focusing on the size and the composition of the balance sheet, focusing on the long-term goals, and not being comfortable with initiating reinvestments until such time as you are really comfortable with the idea of raising the effective lower bound on interest rates—those things should be connected in our thinking.	96	9
201507	1145	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1146	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9
201507	1147	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	96	9
201507	1148	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1149	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	Narayana is quite right.	96	9
201507	1150	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	He, Eric, and a few other people over time have alluded to the possible desirability of an end state in which we have a larger balance sheet and one that may have different-duration securities in it.	96	9
201507	1151	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	It feels like the next several meetings are going to be potentially full enough that we probably would not have enough time to debate something like that.	96	9
201507	1152	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	But should we try to set, at some point, a discussion of that particular issue a little bit apart from the immediacy of decisions about liftoff?	96	9
201507	1153	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Let me just say that the staff have embarked on a project that will concern our long-run framework and will take up these questions, and I planned at the end of the meeting to tell you a little bit more about that work plan.	96	9
201507	1154	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	But I think that is very important.	96	9

201507	1156	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	We’ll be giving you a bit more information about what our expectations are.	96	9

201507	1158	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Powell.	96	9
201507	1159	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1160	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I want to start by distinguishing between two things.	96	9
201507	1161	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	First, there is clearly a need to have a decent answer to the question of when reinvestments will end, and that need will ripen no later than the liftoff press conference.	96	9
201507	1162	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Second, there is the need to decide a whole range of questions about the exact timing, whether we do it all at once or taper, whether we distinguish between Treasury securities and MBS, and whether the taper is a complex or a simple one.	96	9
201507	1163	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	And most or all of that, I think—though we are talking about it today, and it will go into the minutes and season people’s thinking—is not ready to be decided and won’t be for some time.	96	9
201507	1164	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	The way I think about this is that one of the purposes of the first statement, what will be said at the press conference, is to clear space and time to allow the normalization process to proceed and give the Committee the information it needs to make those decisions when they need to be made.	96	9
201507	1165	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Today the public seems to think that the Committee intends to wait somewhere in the range of 6 to 12 months before ending reinvestments either through a taper or perhaps all at once.	96	9
201507	1166	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	The primary dealer survey suggests a reasonable likelihood that we would taper over the course of a year.	96	9
201507	1167	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Those are pretty reasonable parameters, they could be about right, but I don’t see how it is in the Committee’s interest to harden those expectations in the near term.	96	9
201507	1168	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I’ll offer two reasons for caution.	96	9
201507	1169	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	First, the model shows only a very slight effect on rates from ending reinvestment.	96	9
201507	1170	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Actual effects may differ materially from those in the model.	96	9
201507	1171	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	And, as many have noted, I think we learned that lesson—and shouldn’t forget it—in the taper tantrum, when we sat around this table, and I don’t know that there were more people who thought it would be seen as a tightening move or not.	96	9
201507	1172	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I will speak for myself—I certainly didn’t see it as predicting the move that we saw in the summer of 2013.	96	9
201507	1173	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	These signaling effects are not in the model, and they can be very large or not.	96	9
201507	1174	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I don’t expect that there would be much of a reaction from a decision to taper, but I don’t see any reason to take that risk unnecessarily.	96	9
201507	1175	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	The second reason to wait is that I would really like to minimize the chances of ever having to vote again at the zero lower bound on asset purchases.	96	9
201507	1176	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I think both of those reasons argue for caution.	96	9
201507	1177	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I think there’s even a third reason that hasn’t been talked about as much, and that is, the situation has really changed since our earlier discussions on reinvestment.	96	9
201507	1178	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Specifically, the normalization process has slowed down.	96	9
201507	1179	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Liftoff is going to be later than expected, and the pace of increases is going to be slower.	96	9
201507	1180	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I looked back: The median interest rate forecast in the June 2014 SEP showed the policy rate at 1⅛ percent at year-end 2015 and 2½ percent at year-end 2016.	96	9
201507	1181	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Current expectations are far below that.	96	9
201507	1182	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Of course, the reason why liftoff is later and the path is lower is that in June 2014, the central tendency for 2015 economic growth was 3.0 to 3.2 percent, compared with 1.8 to 2.0 percent in the June 2015 SEP.	96	9
201507	1183	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	What has happened is, as global economic growth has weakened, other central banks around the world have cut their rates to their effective lower bound, and they seem likely to remain there for several years.	96	9
201507	1184	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	They’ve also embarked on their own asset purchases.	96	9
201507	1185	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	And all of this is likely to limit significantly the speed with which we can depart from our own effective lower bound.	96	9
201507	1186	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	And unless and until global economic growth moves up significantly, we’re very likely to be forced into a shallow path for rate increases, which is risky but is still preferable to the alternative.	96	9
201507	1187	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I don’t want balance sheet tightening to make that path any shallower, so it makes sense to me that balance sheet tightening should slow down, just like the other pieces of the normalization process.	96	9
201507	1188	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	As a result, when it becomes appropriate to clarify the Committee’s thinking on reinvestment, I would recommend the Committee stress that our focus for now is on the path of the federal funds rate and give only a much more general statement of conditions for shrinking the balance sheet.	96	9
201507	1189	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I would be clearly in the qualitative state-dependent camp.	96	9
201507	1190	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	An example would be, “The Committee expects to begin the process of normalizing the balance sheet when it is reasonably confident that the policy normalization process is well established,” which is very much along the lines of what the Chair, I believe, has said.	96	9
201507	1191	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I have no attraction to calendar-based guidance, nor do I have any attraction to quantitative state-based guidance along the lines of the thresholds that we used earlier.	96	9
201507	1192	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	When we do begin the balance sheet normalization process, I might prefer a phased-in approach to a sudden stop in reinvestments, and I might want to consider distinguishing between Treasury securities and MBS.	96	9
201507	1193	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I think all of those things are going to be informed by events after liftoff, and I find it very hard to have a thoughtful opinion on them until I see where the normalization process is.	96	9
201507	1194	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	I will close by echoing President Lockhart’s earlier point about keeping it simple when it comes time to taper.	96	9
201507	1195	2015-07-29	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1196	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	96	9
201507	1197	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Brainard.	96	9
201507	1198	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1199	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The policy framework that we have agreed to over the past year is designed to make the federal funds rate the focal point for our policy stance and to make the IOER the primary instrument for influencing that rate, with the ON RRP a secondary tool.	96	9
201507	1200	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	That policy framework has the virtue of being clear and familiar to market participants and the public as well as empirically well understood.	96	9
201507	1201	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In contrast, we have far less experience and little quantitative research to guide us on how reductions in the size of the balance sheet away from the effective lower bound would translate into financial conditions.	96	9
201507	1202	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	For that reason, while it is important to reduce the size of the balance sheet over time, I would not want to do so prematurely if that risked any sacrifice to the efficacy of our policy framework.	96	9
201507	1203	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I would prefer the ceasing of reinvestments to be conditioned on economic developments and based on quantitative rather than qualitative guidance.	96	9
201507	1204	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	There have been considerable time and effort over the past few meetings spent informing the public that monetary policy will be conditioned on economic developments.	96	9
201507	1205	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	As reinvestments are an important component of policy, it would be confusing for guidance in this area to be conditioned on the calendar.	96	9
201507	1206	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Quantitative guidance would be clearer and more informative than qualitative guidance.	96	9
201507	1207	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	However, guidance based on one or more macroeconomic indicators would be, I think, problematic and confusing vis-à-vis the macroeconomic framework we have set out for the federal funds rate.	96	9
201507	1208	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The simple solution that others have alluded to is to base reinvestment policy on the federal funds rate, which parsimoniously summarizes the Committee’s view on all macroeconomic factors that influence the outlook for the labor market and inflation.	96	9
201507	1209	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In addition, conditioning on the federal funds rate maintains the focus on our primary policy tool, simplifying communications.	96	9
201507	1210	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	A further advantage is that it guarantees that changes in the IOER and the phaseout of reinvestments will be aligned with one another, minimizing any confusion that might arise from changes to one tool that are not coordinated simultaneously with changes in the setting of the other tool.	96	9
201507	1211	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Regarding the specifics of the guidance to be provided, my preference would be for our communication to state that an initial reduction in reinvestments would likely occur only after the federal funds rate has reached a range around 2 percent.	96	9
201507	1212	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	When rates get into that range, moreover, it may be useful to pause changes to the IOER in order to gauge the effect on financial conditions.	96	9
201507	1213	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	The preferred range would be motivated, in my thinking, by several factors.	96	9
201507	1214	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	First, like many others around this table, I place a high priority on minimizing the risk that adverse economic developments cause a return to the zero lower bound.	96	9
201507	1215	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Maintaining investments until the federal funds rate is within more normal levels and comfortably above the zero lower bound would minimize that risk.	96	9
201507	1216	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Now, as we’ve discussed, the model-based analysis suggests that the direct effect of a delay in reinvestments—until the federal funds rate is within more normal levels—on the term premium and, through longer-term rates, on economic activity is likely to be quite small.	96	9
201507	1217	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Nonetheless, I’m concerned about the signaling effects of an early change in reinvestment policy, which could produce larger effects than we anticipate.	96	9
201507	1218	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In particular, one risk that I would want to keep in mind as we contemplate our reinvestment policy is the risk that longer-run rates behave in an unexpected way that complicates policy, as happened during the taper tantrum.	96	9
201507	1219	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Thus, I prefer to wait on ceasing or tapering until it becomes clear that the headwinds that have limited economic growth to quite moderate levels despite extremely accommodative monetary policy have subsided.	96	9
201507	1220	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I recognize that agreeing to specific quantitative guidance may be difficult, and I’m open to qualitative alternatives such as the ones that have been stated around this table—possibly referring to a time when the federal funds rate returns to more normal levels, or referring to the wish to minimize the risk of a return to the zero lower bound, or simply referencing normalization.	96	9
201507	1221	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I think the difficulty, though, with any qualitative guidance is that market participants may well interpret it as much sooner or later that we intend, and it will immediately leave Committee members to face ongoing efforts to get us to further elaborate what precisely we mean by those qualitative conditions.	96	9
201507	1222	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	In an abundance of caution, I would probably favor some phasing or tapering so as to avoid any possible effects on market function when the time comes.	96	9
201507	1223	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	I might also be inclined to apply the phaseout of reinvestments symmetrically to MBS and Treasury securities, although I must say I’d be open to altering this policy if market conditions suggest it would be desirable to do so when we get to that point.	96	9
201507	1224	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Finally, I do favor communicating clearly our policy framework once these decisions have been made, but I would caution about how we reflect this discussion in the minutes, in order not to get ahead of the policymaking process.	96	9
201507	1225	2015-07-29	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	96	9
201507	1226	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you, and thanks to everyone for a very rich discussion.	96	9
201507	1227	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	I think this has really been quite an interesting set of considerations we’ve discussed.	96	9
201507	1228	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	You’ve heard various suggestions about additional staff work that might be appropriate, and I think we will proceed by sitting down with the staff and trying to narrow down a few alternatives based on what we have heard around the table.	96	9
201507	1229	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	I’m not going to try to summarize the discussion; I think that probably would be a mistake at this point.	96	9
201507	1230	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	But I do hear some congealing of views, and I think we can probably narrow down the alternatives for your consideration and get back to you to have a further discussion on this matter.	96	9
201507	1231	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	I think this has been a really useful discussion, and I thank you for your thoughtfulness.	96	9
201507	1232	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Let’s move along, then, to the fourth item on the agenda, which is “Potential Enhancements to the Summary of Economic Projections.”	92	9
201507	1233	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	And I’d like to call on Governor Fischer to start us off on this topic.	92	9
201507	1234	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201507	1235	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The subcommittee on communications consists of Loretta Mester, Jay Powell, John Williams, and myself, and we have been considering various enhancements to the SEP.	92	9
201507	1236	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Today we are coming to you with a fairly modest initial proposal, which mainly consists of adding medians to the SEP materials in September.	92	9
201507	1237	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	I wasn’t here when FOMC participants considered SEP enhancements in January 2013, but the transcript suggests that adding medians of participants’ responses was the most popular of the proposed changes at that time.	92	9
201507	1238	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The subcommittee continues to think it’s a good idea, and I hope that most of you will think so, too.	92	9
201507	1239	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	You received a memo last week on this topic written by Todd Clark from the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland and Ellen Meade from the Board staff, who are sitting opposite us.	92	9
201507	1240	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	It was a short memo, as these memos go, and so perhaps you all had a chance to read it.	92	9
201507	1241	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	[Laughter]	92	9
201507	1242	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	In a moment, Todd is going to review for you the subcommittee’s proposal and some of our ongoing work, and afterward we’ll have time for any remaining questions or suggestions you may have.	92	9
201507	1243	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Todd, please.	92	9
201507	1244	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Thank you, Governor Fischer.	92	9
201507	1245	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	I will be referring to the handout titled “Materials for Briefing on Potential Enhancements to the Summary of Economic Projections.”	92	9
201507	1246	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	That handout includes a first page that has an outline of my comments and then some graphics that correspond exactly to those included in the staff memo circulated to the Committee last week.	92	9
201507	1247	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	As Governor Fischer indicated, and as the cover memo from the subcommittee indicated, the subcommittee has identified two broad, promising enhancements to the SEP.	92	9
201507	1248	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The first is the addition of median projections, and the second would be the addition of fan charts to illustrate forecast uncertainty.	92	9
201507	1249	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	At this time, as I will detail, the subcommittee is recommending the addition of medians and one other change to the SEP, which concerns the federal funds rate projections in figure 2.	92	9
201507	1250	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The subcommittee intends to come back to the Committee later this year with a recommendation regarding fan charts, and I will conclude with a brief review of staff work on those fan charts.	92	9
201507	1251	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	In their current form, table 1 and figure 1 of the SEP provide the full range and the central tendencies for the macroeconomic variables that are included in the projections.	92	9
201507	1252	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	In the absence of a median or some other central value, public commentary has tended to focus on the midpoints of the central tendencies as measures of participants’ collective view of the outlook.	92	9
201507	1253	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Figure 2 of the SEP provides the “dot plot” for the federal funds rate.	92	9
201507	1254	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	This dot plot does not report a median projection, but it is pretty easy to identify the median projection, and public commentary makes some use of that median.	92	9
201507	1255	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Finally, in the full SEP materials that are published three weeks later along with the FOMC meeting minutes, figure 3 provides histograms for all the macroeconomic variables and the federal funds rate.	92	9
201507	1256	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	And from those histograms, medians can be computed for each of those variables.	92	9
201507	1257	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The basic rationale for including medians in the SEP would be to improve the Committee’s communications by providing, in a more direct way, a measure of participants’ collective view of the outlook and appropriate policy.	92	9
201507	1258	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The median is a robust statistical measure of the center of a distribution.	92	9
201507	1259	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	It is more standard than the midpoint of the central tendency that some of the public uses today.	92	9
201507	1260	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Publishing the median as a summary measure of the collective view would help illuminate that collective view and would foster the public use of that median in summarizing the Committee’s projections.	92	9
201507	1261	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	To incorporate medians, the subcommittee recommends revising both table 1 and figure 1 of the SEP.	92	9
201507	1262	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The proposed new table 1, which is shown on page 2 of the handout, adds columns that would provide the median projections.	92	9
201507	1263	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	It also includes the federal funds rate as a memo item and an expanded title that makes clear the conditioning of the projections on participants’ assessments of appropriate policy.	92	9
201507	1264	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Since we prepared these materials, President Lacker has offered a suggestion that the subcommittee believes to be helpful, and that would be the insertion of the word “projected” before “appropriate” in three places:  In the memo line, instead of “appropriate policy path,” it would be “projected appropriate policy path,” and then the same “projected appropriate” would appear in the first red sentence in the notes to the tables.	92	9
201507	1265	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The point of President Lacker’s suggestion was to emphasize the endogeneity of policy—that there is a reaction function, and policy will respond as the outlook changes, et cetera.	92	9
201507	1266	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The subcommittee believes that President Lacker’s suggestion will be a useful change.	92	9
201507	1267	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Finally, in terms of medians, in the proposed figure 1, which is shown on page 3 of the handout, thick red lines have been added to report the median projection in the case of each of the macroeconomic variables.	92	9
201507	1268	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The subcommittee recommends implementing these changes with the September 2015 SEP.	92	9
201507	1269	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The minutes of the July meeting will report on the discussion of medians that we are about to have, and those minutes could also review the proposed addition and its rationale, along with a summary of the discussion that occurs today.	92	9
201507	1270	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	If participants favor adding the median, then over the intermeeting period, staff will develop and provide complete mockups of the full set of materials—including tables, charts, and text—that would be warranted by the addition of medians.	92	9
201507	1271	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The other change to the SEP the subcommittee is recommending today concerns figure 2, which is shown in its current form on page 4 of the handout.	92	9
201507	1272	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The top panel provides a histogram of participants’ views on the appropriate year of liftoff.	92	9
201507	1273	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The bottom panel provides the dot plot.	92	9
201507	1274	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The start of normalization will make the top panel obsolete, and the subcommittee recommends removing that top panel at that time.	92	9
201507	1275	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The dot plot, on the other hand, in the bottom panel has been useful for communicating policy views in the period since policy has been constrained by the zero lower bound.	92	9
201507	1276	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Looking ahead, the subcommittee believes the dot plot is likely to remain useful for communicating policy and recommends retaining it even after we’re well away from the effective lower bound.	92	9
201507	1277	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Finally, the subcommittee believes that adding fan charts to the SEP would help communicate the considerable uncertainty that attends the Committee’s projections.	92	9
201507	1278	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	The staff is currently working on fan charts that would be centered on the median projections, with uncertainty bands constructed based on historical forecast errors from a range of public and private sources.	92	9
201507	1279	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Fan charts of this form would provide a quantitative illustration of the historical uncertainty that would complement the judgmental assessment of uncertainty that the SEP currently includes.	92	9
201507	1280	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Fan charts would likely be more effective for communicating the historical uncertainty than the root mean squared errors that are currently provided in a table in the SEP and thereby should help to reduce the public’s occasional confusion of forecast uncertainty with the range of views across FOMC participants.	92	9
201507	1281	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	In light of these potential benefits, the subcommittee plans to come back to the Committee with a concrete proposal later this year after resolving some technical issues, some of which require further investigation by the staff.	92	9
201507	1282	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	That concludes my prepared remarks.	92	9
201507	1283	2015-07-29	meeting	CLARK	Ellen and I would be happy to answer any questions you might have.	92	9

201507	1300	2015-07-29	meeting	MESTER	I think it could be an important enhancement to our communications.	92	9
201507	1301	2015-07-29	meeting	MESTER	We all know that the divergence of views across participants is not the same thing as uncertainty, but we’ve seen discussions of this in the press and in the public in which that often is the way the public interprets the divergence of views.	92	9
201507	1302	2015-07-29	meeting	MESTER	And so if we can think about a better way of doing that in terms of conveying uncertainty, I think it would be worthwhile.	92	9

201507	1445	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	We are now going to turn to the economic and financial situation, and let me call on David Wilcox to start us off on our briefings.	11	1
201507	1446	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Thank you, Madam Chair.	11	1
201507	1447	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	I’ll be referring to the packet titled “The US Outlook.”	11	1
201507	1448	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Our view of the macroeconomic situation has not changed very much since the time of the June meeting.	11	1
201507	1449	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	As shown in panel 1 of your first exhibit, we continue to think that real GDP growth stepped up in the second quarter, partly reflecting some unwinding of its very weak first-quarter performance.	11	1
201507	1450	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Even with the rebound, we estimate that GDP growth over the first half of the year averaged only a subpar 1 percent at an annual rate.	11	1
201507	1451	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	During the second half of the year, and as in the June projection, we see GDP growth running in the neighborhood of 2 percent.	11	1
201507	1452	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	By the end of the medium term, the level of real GDP in our projection is slightly lower than in the June Tealbook, reflecting a stronger exchange value of the dollar and slower expected growth of state and local spending.	11	1
201507	1453	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	The incoming labor market data have also been broadly in line with our June forecast.	11	1
201507	1454	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Although the June unemployment rate—figuring into the second-quarter average shown in panel 2—was one-tenth lower than we had anticipated, the labor force participation rate also surprised us to the downside, leaving the employment-to-population ratio close to our previous projection.	11	1
201507	1455	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Likewise, recent gains in private payrolls have come in about as expected.	11	1
201507	1456	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	As we noted in the Tealbook, the combination of the June decline in the unemployment rate and the slightly weaker outlook for real GDP, all else being equal, would have left us showing no further reduction in the unemployment rate between last month and the end of 2017—an outcome that, in our view, would not have balanced the risks associated with our unemployment rate forecast.	11	1
201507	1457	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	To better balance the risks, we made some small adjustments to our supply-side assumptions.	11	1
201507	1458	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	In particular, we lowered our estimate of the natural rate of unemployment 0.1 percentage point, to 5.1 percent.	11	1
201507	1459	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	We also trimmed our forecast for potential output growth 0.1 percentage point per year, and we did not let that latter adjustment show through into our outlook for aggregate demand.	11	1
201507	1460	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Taken together—and holding everything else equal—these two adjustments generate a forecast for the unemployment rate that is 0.2 percentage point lower than it otherwise would have been.	11	1
201507	1461	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Half of that amount reflects a tighter economy, and half reflects a better- functioning labor market.	11	1
201507	1462	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	The argument for a lower natural rate is bolstered by the continued aging of the labor force.	11	1
201507	1463	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Although we had recognized a potential role for demographics for some time, until now we have been wary about letting their effect show through too strongly to our post-recession natural rate estimates.	11	1
201507	1464	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Panel 6, which uses the JOLTS data to plot a version of the Beveridge curve, suggests why:  Since the previous business cycle peak, this version of the Beveridge curve has shifted out—a development that could be taken as evidence that labor market functioning had been persistently damaged by the Great Recession.	11	1
201507	1465	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	But we have also been influenced by recent analysis by two Board economists—	11	1
201507	1466	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Andrew Figura and David Ratner—that provides some evidence that the outward shift in the Beveridge curve may not have coincided with an increase in the natural rate.	11	1
201507	1467	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	In fact, they argue that if the large secular decline in labor’s share of income has been due to a reduction in the bargaining power of workers, then the natural rate might actually be similar to levels seen before the crisis despite the outward shift in the Beveridge curve.	11	1
201507	1468	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	In other words, the Figura/Ratner analysis casts greater doubt on one of the considerations that had been impeding us from marking down the natural rate.	11	1
201507	1469	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Regarding the amount of slack that remains to be taken up at present, panel 3 provides updated estimates of three different measures of resource utilization that we follow.	11	1
201507	1470	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	The dotted blue line shows the judgmental estimate that is reflected in the Tealbook baseline.	11	1
201507	1471	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	The red line gives the production-function variant of the output gap from the EDO model, which is one of our DSGE models.	11	1
201507	1472	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	And the black line— labeled FRB/US—plots an estimate of slack that pools the information from a number of production, labor market, and price indicators while explicitly allowing for measurement error.	11	1
201507	1473	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Both the Tealbook and EDO point estimates suggest that resource utilization has nearly returned to its sustainable position, while the FRB/US estimate suggests that it is already there.	11	1
201507	1474	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Meanwhile, the substantial confidence interval around the FRB/US values strongly suggests that precision is not the greatest hallmark of these estimates.	11	1
201507	1475	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	With regard to prices, as you can see from panel 4, we have revised down our near-term forecast for total PCE inflation.	11	1
201507	1476	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Virtually all of this revision reflects a sharper expected decline in consumer energy prices over the second half of this year that, in turn, results from the recent step-down in oil prices.	11	1
201507	1477	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Although the exact timing of the pass-through from oil prices to retail energy prices is uncertain, our best estimate is that we will see some noticeable declines in gasoline prices over the next few months.	11	1
201507	1478	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	For core inflation—panel 5—the incoming data have been broadly in line with our expectations.	11	1
201507	1479	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Next year and in 2017, our projections of both total and core inflation rates are little changed from June.	11	1
201507	1480	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Finally, I asked Jeremy Rudd to prepare an additional exhibit that provides a high- level summary of the information that will be available to you at your September, October, and December meetings.	11	1
201507	1481	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Incidentally, I think of this as taking up a challenge that President Lockhart issued a few meetings ago to try to envision more concretely the information set that would be available to the Committee.	11	1
201507	1482	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Specifically, the data releases that will be available at the September FOMC meeting are in the blue-shaded region, the incremental releases that will have become available by the time of the October meeting are in the yellow-shaded region, and the observations that will be available for your December meeting are shown in red.	11	1
201507	1483	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	In addition, we’ve indicated the readings that would be consistent with our baseline forecast.	11	1
201507	1484	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	As you can see, for the September meeting, you will have PCE inflation data through July—though the August CPI will be released on the first day of the meeting—and labor market data through August.	11	1
201507	1485	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	October will afford you just one more reading on each of these monthly indicators.	11	1
201507	1486	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	And December will give you two more beyond that.	11	1
201507	1487	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	On the real activity front, we expect to see continued solid payroll gains at a pace that, on average, is not too different from the average seen over the first half of this year.	11	1
201507	1488	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	One thing I would note, however, is that based on recent historical experience, there is some risk that the initial August reading on payroll employment growth will be softer than we’ve penciled in as our expectation for the underlying reality and will then be revised up in subsequent months.	11	1
201507	1489	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Last year, for example, job gains in August were initially estimated at 142,000, a figure that was revised up to 203,000 in the subsequent two releases.	11	1
201507	1490	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	More generally, over the past five years, the initial estimate for August has been revised up 75,000 on average.	11	1
201507	1491	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	On the inflation side, we expect the data readings you will have in hand to stay low even through the time of the December meeting.	11	1
201507	1492	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	In the baseline forecast, we now have the three-month change in total PCE prices stepping down noticeably after the September meeting, reflecting the projected energy price declines I mentioned earlier.	11	1
201507	1493	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Core inflation is expected to run slightly below 1½ percent, with the three-month changes expected to be held down some by residual seasonality, which looks to us to weigh most heavily on core inflation in the latter part of this year.	11	1
201507	1494	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	Having shown you these values that would be consistent with our baseline forecast, I hasten to note that however murky you may perceive the message of this exhibit to be, the message of the actual incoming data could be a good deal murkier.	11	1
201507	1495	2015-07-29	meeting	WILCOX	The reason is because those incoming data will contain all the features that real-world data have, including measurement error, seasonal adjustment problems, mutual inconsistency, normal random variation, and even substantive departures from our current baseline forecast.	11	1

201507	1499	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	To continue the reference to annoyances, one of the annoyances of summer driving—say, to the beach or mountains on a holiday weekend—is traffic jams.	11	1
201507	1500	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	It’s especially annoying when, for no apparent reason, traffic grinds to a halt, trudges over the next mile and a half at a glacial pace, and then, with no car crash or other distraction in sight, mysteriously speeds back up again.	11	1
201507	1501	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	We’ve had much the same feeling watching developments in Greece.	11	1
201507	1502	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	At the time of your most recent meeting, negotiations between Greece and its creditors had been moving forward, albeit quite contentiously.	11	1
201507	1503	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Then talks broke down, the banks were closed, a payment to the IMF was missed, and Greek voters resoundingly rejected the creditors’ proposals.	11	1
201507	1504	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But then, after marathon all-night negotiations, Greece’s Prime Minister Tsipras agreed to even stricter austerity measures than voters had rejected the week before.	11	1
201507	1505	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And as suddenly as they stopped, talks on the adjustment and financing program started up again.	11	1
201507	1506	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Details of the program are still being hammered out, but it looks to provide up to €85 billion in loans over three years, conditional on the government implementing a broad array of fiscal and structural reforms.	11	1
201507	1507	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Most of the money won’t stay in Greece.	11	1
201507	1508	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Although on the order of €25 billion will be used to recapitalize the badly damaged banking system, nearly €55 billion goes out the door to service earlier loans from the IMF and European creditors.	11	1
201507	1509	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Even with this assistance, Greece will likely be required to run primary budget surpluses—that is, budget balances excluding interest payments—of 1½ percent of GDP in the next two years, rising to 3½ percent in 2018.	11	1
201507	1510	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	This burden will be very difficult to bear, considering that Greek GDP—panel 1— will almost certainly plummet this quarter, and that the current government is far from committed to the austerity measures it has promised.	11	1
201507	1511	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Accordingly, Greece will almost certainly run into problems fulfilling the program’s conditions over the next few years, leading to further difficulties with its creditors and potentially even to the outcome that Greece just narrowly avoided: its exit from the euro area.	11	1
201507	1512	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	While the developments of the past month have further worsened Greece’s outlook, they’ve had little effect on prospects for the euro area more generally.	11	1
201507	1513	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	As Simon has described, financial markets outside Greece were little affected.	11	1
201507	1514	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And indicators of euro-area economic activity have generally pointed to continued recovery.	11	1
201507	1515	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	As shown in panel 2, we’ve marked down economic growth ¼ percentage point in the third quarter, but that solely reflects the arithmetic effects of Greece’s deep contraction on euro-area GDP.	11	1
201507	1516	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Beyond the near term, we’ve actually boosted the forecast a touch to reflect lower oil prices and the weaker euro exchange rate.	11	1
201507	1517	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But there remains a question of whether Greece’s debacle may have weakened confidence in the resilience of the monetary union and rendered it more vulnerable to subsequent disturbances.	11	1
201507	1518	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The answer is not obvious.	11	1
201507	1519	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	On the one hand, it is reassuring that spillovers from the Greek turmoil were so muted.	11	1
201507	1520	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	On the other hand, the disorganization and disunity evidenced by European authorities, as well as their resistance to directly address the sustainability of Greece’s debt, may bode poorly for the region’s response to future crises.	11	1
201507	1521	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Furthermore, the recent deterioration in Greece’s economic and financial situation has likely diminished its chances for normalizing its situation within the euro area.	11	1
201507	1522	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	In addition to Greece, of course, the correction of the Chinese stock market— panel 3—has attracted considerable attention in recent weeks.	11	1
201507	1523	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But here, too, the sound and fury signified relatively little for our forecast.	11	1
201507	1524	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	To begin with, the fall in equity prices didn’t reveal any fundamental underlying weaknesses that we did not already appreciate.	11	1
201507	1525	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The downturn after the prior surge in stocks looks like a classic example of a bursting bubble.	11	1
201507	1526	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Additionally, because of the relatively small weight of the Chinese stock market in household wealth, corporate financing, and overall financial assets, we do not believe the correction will weigh heavily on China’s future economic growth prospects.	11	1
201507	1527	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	It also bears noting that Chinese stock prices remain well above last year’s levels, though this may, in part, reflect government actions to support the market.	11	1
201507	1528	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	To be sure, China’s financial system is exposed to a number of significant vulnerabilities, including weakened property markets, extremely high debt levels, and a shadow banking sector that remains opaque and poorly regulated.	11	1
201507	1529	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Accordingly, it is possible that the stock market crash could trigger a chain reaction of margin calls, fire sales, and defaults that leads to broader financial and economic disruptions.	11	1
201507	1530	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But that does not strike us as the most likely scenario.	11	1
201507	1531	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	In our baseline forecast, Chinese GDP growth, having bounced up to nearly 8 percent in the second quarter, moderates over the remainder of the forecast period, in line with declining potential output growth.	11	1
201507	1532	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Although the recent developments in neither Greece nor China made a strong imprint on the outlook, the tone of foreign economic activity appears a little weaker than we had previously assessed.	11	1
201507	1533	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	As shown in panel 4, economic growth in both the emerging market economies and advanced foreign economies had fallen sharply in the first quarter, but at the time of the June Tealbook—the dashed lines—we had assumed growth would rebound in the second quarter.	11	1
201507	1534	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	In the event, recent data on trade, production, and PMIs have been considerably weaker than we’d anticipated, and we have lowered our near-term projections of real GDP growth accordingly.	11	1
201507	1535	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	We still judge most of these downdrafts to be temporary.	11	1
201507	1536	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	In particular, output in Canada, which occupies a large trade weight in our foreign aggregate, was partly depressed by wildfires and maintenance shutdowns in the energy sector.	11	1
201507	1537	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And already, June PMIs have moved up.	11	1
201507	1538	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Therefore, we have foreign economic growth rebounding in the coming quarters, and our medium-term outlook is little changed.	11	1
201507	1539	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	However, indicators of economic activity and trade came in softer than we expected for many countries besides Canada, and we remain attuned to the risk that the first-half pothole may turn into a more extended slowdown.	11	1
201507	1540	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The continued weak tone of foreign activity likely contributed to a number of developments affecting the US economy, as Simon has described.	11	1
201507	1541	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	First, as shown in panel 5, oil prices fell $11 per barrel over the intermeeting period, though the decline also reflected still-high levels of oil production and expectations for a step-up in Iranian oil exports following the completion of the nuclear deal.	11	1
201507	1542	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Second, as shown in panel 6, the dollar rose further, likely reflecting continued focus on the divergence between monetary policy prospects in the United States and abroad.	11	1
201507	1543	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The rise in the dollar since last summer has been an important factor weighing on the outlook for US net exports and, thus, on economic activity.	11	1
201507	1544	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Accordingly, I thought I’d spend a few minutes explaining how we devise our trade forecast and why we are predicting so much drag from the dollar.	11	1
201507	1545	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	As indicated in your next exhibit, at the heart of our forecast is a model composed of a number of equations for the main categories of exports and imports of goods and services.	11	1
201507	1546	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	These equations explain the evolution of these trade volumes based on movements in their fundamental drivers: foreign and US GDP and real exchange rates.	11	1
201507	1547	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	They are econometrically estimated using more than 35 years of quarterly data, and they are simulated using staff forecasts for the explanatory variables.	11	1
201507	1548	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	These model projections are then augmented by historical trends and judgmental forecasts for several additional trade categories.	11	1
201507	1549	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The bottom line is that, based on our trade model, we assess that a 10 percent rise in the broad real dollar should lead to a deterioration in the contribution of net exports to US GDP of 1.7 percent.	11	1
201507	1550	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Importantly, our econometric estimates identify significant lags in the transmission of the dollar to US trade, so this 1.7 percent drag takes place over a period of three years.	11	1
201507	1551	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The middle panel of your exhibit compares the actual evolution of the net export contribution to US GDP growth (the solid black line) with our model projection, decomposed into the part coming from the dollar (the blue bars), US and foreign GDP (the green bars), and our separate forecast for the volume of oil imports (the purple bars).	11	1
201507	1552	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Looking at the far right of this chart, notice that our forecast of the net export drag coming from the dollar in 2015 and through 2017 totals about 2 percentage points.	11	1
201507	1553	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	This drag essentially reflects the 1.7 percent elasticity coming from our trade model that I mentioned earlier, combined with a rise in the broad real dollar since last summer amounting to 12 percent, as shown in the bottom panel.	11	1
201507	1554	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Turning to the historical portion of the chart, our model does a reasonably good job of tracking previous periods of large dollar appreciation and net exports deterioration as well as periods of dollar depreciation and trade improvement.	11	1
201507	1555	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	The erratic swings in net exports since the global financial crisis have been difficult to track, but we see no evidence that the basic behavior of net exports has shifted significantly from earlier years.	11	1
