201507	1558	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	My briefing summarizes the staff’s current assessment of US financial stability.	FS	FS
201507	1559	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	I’ll be referring to charts in the handout titled “Financial Stability Developments.”	FS	FS
201507	1560	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The picture is largely unchanged from last quarter, though we have nudged up the level of vulnerabilities stemming from asset valuation pressures and business sector leverage.	FS	FS
201507	1561	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	These vulnerabilities, however, continue to be counterbalanced by relatively high levels of capital and moderate levels of maturity transformation in the financial system.	FS	FS
201507	1562	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	On net, we judge the vulnerability of the financial system as a whole to be in the moderate range, a level determined relative to metrics covering roughly the past 30 years.	FS	FS
201507	1563	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The remainder of my briefing provides some detail on this overall assessment.	FS	FS
201507	1564	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As shown in the top-left panel, capital and liquidity positions of bank holding companies are substantially higher than in the period preceding the crisis, increasing the stability of the core of the financial system.	FS	FS
201507	1565	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Common equity tier 1 capital, the red line, has been about unchanged over the past year even as implementation of Basel III has led to a rise in risk-weighted assets.	FS	FS
201507	1566	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, firms have built up their ratio of high-quality liquid assets, the black line, and now most meet their new, fully phased- in liquidity requirements.	FS	FS
201507	1567	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In the nonbank sector, signs related to leverage are mixed.	FS	FS
201507	1568	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Dealers’ net borrowing for fixed-income securities, the blue line in the right panel, has continued to decline to a very low level, and capital ratios, not shown, have been rising.	FS	FS
201507	1569	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	On the other hand, margin debt for equities as a share of GDP is at a record level, and responses to the SCOOS, the Senior Credit Officer Opinion Survey, and conversations with market participants indicate wider use of “synthetic prime brokerage” arrangements by hedge funds, suggesting some pickup in off-balance-sheet leverage.	FS	FS
201507	1570	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	On net, though, we judge that vulnerabilities from the overall financial-sector leverage to be at a below-average level.	FS	FS
201507	1571	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As Simon discussed earlier, the imprint of recent financial stresses related to Greece and China on the US financial system has been limited.	FS	FS
201507	1572	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As shown in the middle-left panel, five-year CDS spreads of large US financial institutions were mostly unchanged.	FS	FS
201507	1573	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The muted response to the financial turmoil in Greece is in sharp contrast to the situation in late 2011, when Greek debt problems had escalated, likely reflecting currently lower direct exposures and, potentially, a smaller shock from Greece related to beliefs that European authorities will take actions to stem contagion.	FS	FS
201507	1574	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The current reactions also likely reflect the considerably stronger financial positions of firms that are connected directly or indirectly to one another.	FS	FS
201507	1575	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	A major vulnerability in the previous crisis was the scale of short-term wholesale funding used to finance longer-term risky assets.	FS	FS
201507	1576	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	An aggregate measure of runnable money-like liabilities, shown in the middle-right panel, has fallen significantly from its peak in 2008 as amounts outstanding for most instruments, such as repo and MMFs, have shrunk.	FS	FS
201507	1577	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, there has been important progress to address key structural problems, such as the SEC’s rulemaking to remove the NAV rounding from institutional prime MMFs, and the near-elimination of intraday credit in the triparty repo market.	FS	FS
201507	1578	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	That said, the full effects of the MMF reforms are still to be seen.	FS	FS
201507	1579	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Recently, many market participants have expressed concerns about reduced bond market liquidity—that is, they are not able to transact in reasonable quantities when they want to at reasonably low and predictable costs.	FS	FS
201507	1580	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	A recent staff assessment of transactions in corporate bonds and Treasury securities does not support a significant deterioration in market functioning, but the picture for the resilience of liquidity is somewhat mixed.	FS	FS
201507	1581	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Two representative indicators are shown in the bottom-left panel.	FS	FS
201507	1582	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As shown by the black line, bid-asked spreads for corporate high-yield bonds have been trending down and are relatively low.	FS	FS
201507	1583	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	At the same time, the share of trades that are greater than $1 million, the red line, has declined, indicating average transaction sizes are now smaller.	FS	FS
201507	1584	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In the Treasury market, the recently issued interagency study on the October 15 event detailed a sharp decline in market depth in the narrow event window but did not identify a specific cause for the volatility.	FS	FS
201507	1585	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, it showed that trading volumes were very high, pricing was continuous, and cash and futures markets stayed aligned.	FS	FS
201507	1586	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	It also highlighted that firms employing proprietary high-frequency trading strategies accounted for more than one-half the transactions, and traditional broker-dealers accounted for about one-third of transactions on typical days in 2014, indicating important structural changes in the provision of liquidity.	FS	FS
201507	1587	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	One often expressed reason for heightened concerns about market liquidity is the increase in mutual fund holdings of corporate debt, including relatively illiquid loans and bonds, shown in the lower-right panel.	FS	FS
201507	1588	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The concern is that the offer of daily cash redemptions by open-end mutual funds provides a first-mover advantage for investors to exit ahead of others, who would be left to bear the costs of asset sales.	FS	FS
201507	1589	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Thus, a fall in bond prices could lead to greater-than-expected redemptions and to an increase in price volatility.	FS	FS
201507	1590	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The staff are working (along with the FSOC and FSB) on these possible effects, but they currently believe they would likely not have significant spillovers in the absence of leverage by bond investors.	FS	FS
201507	1591	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The next exhibit starts with asset valuations, an area where we have seen some pressure in some sectors based on indicators being notably above their averages of recent decades.	FS	FS
201507	1592	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Asset valuations are tracked because compressed risk premiums could suggest a higher risk of a sharp drop in prices and a potential systemic risk if interacted with other vulnerabilities, such as high leverage and maturity transformation.	FS	FS
201507	1593	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	They also are an indicator of investor risk appetite because much risk-taking behavior is not easily observable or quantifiable.	FS	FS
201507	1594	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As shown in the top-left panel, corporate bond yields remain near historical lows, reflecting, in part, low term premiums on Treasury securities.	FS	FS
201507	1595	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Spreads also are narrow, though they rose a bit last year as risks for energy firms increased when oil prices fell.	FS	FS
201507	1596	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Low rates have supported strong issuance of high-yield corporate bonds and leveraged loans, as shown in the lower-right panel, and outstanding debt had been risen rapidly.	FS	FS
201507	1597	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, as we have noted in the past, underwriting standards such as debt-to-earnings multiples have been relatively loose, though public data indicate the credit quality of loans has improved this year.	FS	FS
201507	1598	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In commercial real estate, prices have been rising rapidly in the past three years, and the capitalization rate—the rent-to-price ratio, shown by the black line in the middle-left chart—has been falling to a level last seen in 2007.	FS	FS
201507	1599	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, CMBS issuance, the bars in the panel, have been picking up.	FS	FS
201507	1600	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Combined with CRE loans at banks, the ratio of total CRE debt to GDP is above average but has not yet accelerated.	FS	FS
201507	1601	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Competition among lenders reportedly is leading to a weakening of standards, and loan-to-value ratios and the share of interest-only loans in CMBS pools appear to be rising.	FS	FS
201507	1602	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	At commercial banks, lending standards have been easing, on balance, for the past few years, and supervisors have started to increase their monitoring of this sector.	FS	FS
201507	1603	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In the equity markets, forward price-earnings ratios for the S&P 500 and for small cap stocks, shown in the middle-right panel, have edged off their recent highs but are above their historical averages.	FS	FS
201507	1604	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	The equity risk premium, not shown, has continued to decline but is not unusually narrow, largely reflecting the low interest rates.	FS	FS
201507	1605	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Turning to the nonfinancial sector, as shown in the bottom-left panel, real credit growth to households and businesses has been picking up, but this masks distinct trends in the business and household sectors.	FS	FS
201507	1606	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	With the business sector having emerged from the crisis in solid shape, credit has been available on favorable terms for many years, and debt has grown rapidly for the riskier part of the corporate sector.	FS	FS
201507	1607	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	As shown in the bottom-right panel, the debt-to-asset ratio for speculative-grade corporations, the red line, has increased to near-record levels.	FS	FS
201507	1608	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	This ratio is also rising for all nonfinancial corporations, shown by the black line, though debt service burdens are low, held down by low rates and high earnings.	FS	FS
201507	1609	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In contrast, many households have been continuing to work down their debt, and loan balances are rising only modestly.	FS	FS
201507	1610	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	Household debt-to-income ratios, measured at the county level, have moved down significantly in recent years and have yet to turn around.	FS	FS
201507	1611	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In summary, the largest financial firms are now more resilient, and credit for businesses and households is supported by more stable and less complex funding than before the crisis.	FS	FS
201507	1612	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	These developments are central to our overall assessment that the financial system is only moderately prone to amplify shocks.	FS	FS
201507	1613	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	That said, rising debt burdens for riskier businesses, elevated valuations, and loosening lending standards for many assets point to some building pressures and may warrant additional attention if current trends were to persist.	FS	FS
201507	1614	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	In addition, bearers of liquidity risk in the corporate bond market appear to have shifted from highly leveraged firms, the banks and broker-dealers, to much less leveraged entities, such as private funds.	FS	FS
201507	1615	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	This shift, in principle, could be stability enhancing, on balance, but raises questions about the resilience of liquidity and possible spillover effects.	FS	FS
201507	1616	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	For markets more broadly, including both corporate and Treasury securities markets, the staff currently are studying potential changes in the incentives for the provision of liquidity, including changes in technology, disclosure practices, and dealers’ business models because of regulations or changes they may have made on their own to their risk-management practices.	FS	FS
201507	1617	2015-07-29	meeting	LIANG	That concludes our prepared remarks, and we’d be happy to take your questions.	FS	FS

201507	1724	2015-07-29	meeting	LOCKHART	When the dollar began to appreciate a little more than a year ago, a back-of-the-envelope guesstimate that I would have made was that a significant amount of our trade is cross-border with Mexico and Canada, a back-and-forth, supply chain sort of trade, which is not necessarily sensitive to an exchange rate.	12	1

201507	1732	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	I think you are entirely right that the more your trade involves these two- way trades, with inputs going back and forth, that, all else being equal, ought to diminish the effect of exchange rates, at least on gross trade flows.	12	1
201507	1733	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	It will not necessarily diminish their effect on trade balance, because, again, these things are going back and forth.	12	1
201507	1734	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But all else being equal, you might expect some reduced effect of exchange rates on trade the more of your trade is of the two-way type, and the less of it is non-two-way trade in, for example, finished goods.	12	1
201507	1735	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Now, a couple things.	12	1
201507	1736	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	First, we have been very keen to try to ascertain whether our model estimates, which are basically estimated over a sample that goes back to the 1970s in some cases, in some sense apply less now than they did before.	12	1
201507	1737	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And the short answer is that we are not convinced that they are less applicable, though we are parsing some evidence that goes in different directions.	12	1
201507	1738	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	If you take a look at the charts, looking at the years 2010 through 2014, it doesn’t look like our model has gone tremendously awry.	12	1
201507	1739	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	But at the same time, it doesn’t seem to be fitting as well as in previous episodes.	12	1
201507	1740	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Second, it is true that if you estimate our trade models over some more recent samples—say, starting in 2000—then you do get somewhat smaller elasticities.	12	1
201507	1741	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	On the other hand, you don’t get particularly improved performance, in a robust sense, in tracking changes in the trade balance over this period.	12	1
201507	1742	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And we think that a lot of what is going on is that the global financial crisis so disrupted trade flows—it led to this huge decline in trade, followed by very sharp rebounds, that our model couldn’t capture—and that is part of what’s at play.	12	1
201507	1743	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	So, we are alert to the possibility that the structure of trade has changed over time, but we are not convinced we see enough of that in the data to merit reducing our elasticities of the response of trade to the dollar.	12	1
201507	1744	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	Now, directly to that issue, our model embodies responses of exports and imports to changes in the dollar that are not particularly outsized, and broadly speaking are in the neighborhood of what you will find in the literature.	12	1
201507	1745	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And I would note that, as a rule of thumb, macroeconomists often assume that trade responses, or any type of demand responses to prices, are of a unit elasticity.	12	1
201507	1746	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	So if the price goes up 1 percent, the demand goes down 1 percent.	12	1
201507	1747	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And we often make those assumptions in our trade models, too.	12	1
201507	1748	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	In fact, our model incorporates features such that when the dollar goes up 10 percent, exports fall by 7½ percent.	12	1
201507	1749	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	That implies a responsiveness of trade to the dollar that is actually less than 1.	12	1
201507	1750	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	And on the import side, we have even smaller responses to a change in the dollar.	12	1
201507	1751	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	So the merits of further compressing those trade responses don’t seem great.	12	1
201507	1752	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	You raise a question that we are definitely in the process of wrestling with.	12	1
201507	1753	2015-07-29	meeting	KAMIN	For now, we think the trade responses that we have built in are about right, but we are certainly going to be looking into this further.	12	1

201507	1755	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	12	1

201507	1759	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	I’ll take that question.	12	1
201507	1760	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	The chart shows that the Beveridge curve for the JOLTS vacancy rate has shifted out.	12	1
201507	1761	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	Ordinarily one would think of a shift out in the Beveridge curve as indicative of a decline in matching efficiency, and we had built that in.	12	1
201507	1762	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	One of the reasons we hadn’t lowered our natural rate was because we were taking some signal from that, offsetting the downward pressure on the natural rate that arises from demographic changes in the labor force.	12	1
201507	1763	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	So what David was referring to was that Andrew Figura and David Ratner on our staff have put forward an argument that the outward shift in the Beveridge curve may not be reflective of an increase in the natural rate of unemployment if it instead is due to a decline in worker bargaining power, as perhaps evidenced by the decline in the labor share over the past decade or so.	12	1
201507	1764	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	In that case it is more profitable for firms to post a vacancy because the labor share is lower, and they get more of the profits from any additional employee.	12	1
201507	1765	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	And that would push up the equilibrium level of vacancies relative to any unemployment rate.	12	1
201507	1766	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	That is roughly the story that David was trying to illustrate with the Beveridge curve.	12	1
201507	1767	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	That it has shifted out.	12	1
201507	1768	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	Previously, we had interpreted that as consistent with a decline in matching efficiency or an increase in the natural rate.	12	1
201507	1769	2015-07-29	meeting	WASCHER	But maybe there is another story that leads us not to take as much signal from that outward shift.	12	1
201507	1770	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you.	12	1

201507	1861	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	FS2	
201507	1862	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	I thought it might be useful to give a brief discussion of the financial-stability tabletop exercise conducted by the Financial Stability Committee of the Conference of Presidents in Charlotte in June.	FS2	
201507	1863	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The purpose was to obtain a better understanding of how the nexus of monetary policy and supervisory policy tools could be used in a scenario in which financial stability issues were emerging.	FS2	
201507	1864	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The exercise was based on the assumption that decisions were being made in 2017.	FS2	
201507	1865	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	At that time, the scenario that was assumed was that the economy had attained both elements of the dual mandate and the economy was expected to remain at full employment and 2 percent inflation in the absence of any changes to our monetary policy stance.	FS2	
201507	1866	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The staff provided forecasts for both macroeconomic variables using Tealbook discussions as well as developments in financial markets.	FS2	
201507	1867	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The scenario also included signs of emerging financial instability.	FS2	
201507	1868	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Specifically, the scenario included a rapid rise in real estate values, significant growth in the shadow banking sector, and increased risk-taking by non-SIFI financial intermediaries.	FS2	
201507	1869	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The discussion of the scenario and possible policy responses highlighted the important role of both governance issues and time lags in many of the potential supervisory policy responses.	FS2	
201507	1870	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In part because of these governance complications and the lags in supervisory implementation, some committee members thought that monetary policy tools should be considered to address the concerns about future financial stability, even though doing so would result in a less optimal macroeconomic outcome.	FS2	
201507	1871	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Other members wanted to use supervisory tools and margin requirements but did not necessarily rule out using a monetary policy tool at some later point.	FS2	
201507	1872	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The exercise was useful in crystallizing potential financial-stability problems and the complexity of responding to those problems.	FS2	
201507	1873	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	I’ve shared the materials with Governors Fischer and Tarullo and think it might be worthwhile considering such an exercise for the full Committee at some future date.	FS2

201507	1933	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	New England, like most of the country, has continued to see improvement in labor markets.	13	1

201507	1935	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	That’s why it’s surprising that qualitative discussions of labor markets from advisory groups and regional contacts describe wage and compensation pressures as being primarily isolated to difficult-to-fill positions, such as cybersecurity professionals.	13	1
201507	1936	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Even with a low unemployment rate in most New England states, employers aren’t complaining of wage pressures other than those due to legislated salary changes and those in hard-to-fill jobs.	13	1
201507	1937	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In light of the qualitative responses from businesses in our region, it’s been quite puzzling that the employment cost index has recently registered such outsized increases in New England.	13	1
201507	1938	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Nationally, until a year ago, the ECI had been rising at a very modest rate, roughly at 2 percent a year.	13	1
201507	1939	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Over the past three reports, the year-over-year ECI has been trending up and is currently at 2.8 percent nationally, which might be interpreted as showing the first signs in the data of emerging labor market tightness.	13	1
201507	1940	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Interestingly, the regional patterns in the ECI tell a somewhat less straightforward story.	13	1
201507	1941	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In the South, the ECI shows little trend, rising only 2.2 percent on a 12-month basis for each of the past three quarters.	13	1
201507	1942	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The Midwest has shown more of a trend, but for the 12 months ending in the first quarter, it grew only 2.4 percent.	13	1
201507	1943	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In general, other than in the Northeast, the ECI has increased only modestly.	13	1
201507	1944	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	It turns out that a large portion of the increase in 2015:Q1 had to do with a sizable jump in compensation for private industry workers in New England.	13	1
201507	1945	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The seasonally unadjusted quarterly change from the previous quarter was about 20 percent.	13	1
201507	1946	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	On a Q4-over-Q4 basis, the rate of growth was 7½ percent.	13	1
201507	1947	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The increase was even larger for the wages-and-salaries component, indicating the jump was likely not concentrated in benefits.	13	1
201507	1948	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	There are several issues associated with how to interpret the New England ECI reading.	13	1
201507	1949	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	For instance, the jump in the ECI was mostly driven by incentive-paid occupations, such as sales.	13	1
201507	1950	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	It’s dubious, however, that changes in pay for performance from sales commissions reflected genuine change in labor costs rather than just an improvement in demand conditions.	13	1
201507	1951	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In addition, while the BLS did not provide us with the underlying compensation details, in terms of industry and/or occupation in the New England region, it did mention that, in addition to sales, professional business services—namely, computer design, legal services, and scientific and architectural design—were the main drivers of the outsized increase in the first quarter.	13	1
201507	1952	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Other wage data sources, however, do not corroborate such an explanation.	13	1
201507	1953	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	For example, the average hourly earnings for professional business services in Massachusetts does not indicate a jump in earnings in the first quarter of 2015.	13	1
201507	1954	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	It’s hard to think that compositional shifts in this measure would have been rapid and large enough to entirely mask the wage increases in these occupations recorded in the ECI.	13	1
201507	1955	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Another possibility, which would complement the BLS interpretation of the recent ECI reading, is that the minimum wage laws in Massachusetts, Connecticut, Rhode Island, and Vermont have caused a jump for minimum wage workers.	13	1
201507	1956	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	It may also have some effect on supervisors of minimum wage workers or workers whose salary is priced as a spread to the minimum wage.	13	1
201507	1957	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	While this would reflect legislated wage pressure and one-shot level increases rather than demand-driven wage pressures, it might also help explain the New England pattern.	13	1
201507	1958	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	To check this, my staff turned to the Current Population Survey to determine the evolution of wages, both in New England and outside New England, for different percentiles of the respective distributions.	13	1
201507	1959	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Reported hourly wages in New England increased in 2015:Q1 to a greater extent than in other regions.	13	1
201507	1960	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Moreover, the distribution of reported hourly wages in New England shows some clear shift around the minimum wage cutoffs.	13	1
201507	1961	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	But increases in the wages for all New England workers calculated as usual weekly earnings over usual weekly hours were much less anomalous relative to the recent past, suggesting that while it’s possible that minimum wage changes played some role in the ECI increase, such a role was likely modest.	13	1
201507	1962	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In this respect, it’s notable that a disaggregation of CPS usual wage data, in terms of worker skills, does not reveal any special pattern for New England in the first quarter, not even for low-skilled workers.	13	1
201507	1963	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In the end, we’re left with a puzzle.	13	1
201507	1964	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Much of the first-quarter growth in the ECI is concentrated in New England, but other data series and discussions with employers failed to corroborate the increase in the ECI.	13	1
201507	1965	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	While the ECI outside New England is rising, it’s increasing at a much more modest pace.	13	1
201507	1966	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	And even if the New England ECI reading is accurate, it may well be a result of the augmentation of income for certain occupations and/or minimum wage earners, with little economy-wide inflationary consequences.	13	1
201507	1967	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	We hope incoming data will provide more insight into the nature of recent increases in the ECI, but at this point I’d like to see more data before assuming that the overall increase in the ECI is a reflection of broad-based labor demand generated from a tightening of labor market conditions.	13	1
201507	1968	2015-07-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you.	13	1

201507	2265	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Like President Lacker, I found President Rosengren’s comments about the minimum wage and the regional nature of the increases in the employment cost index very interesting.	13	1
201507	2266	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I suppose I viewed at least a significant portion of the increase of the minimum wage as reflecting economic forces.	13	1
201507	2267	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And I know it’s a political decision, but often it reflects labor market conditions, too.	13	1
201507	2268	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	So it’s an interesting issue: to what extent, historically, have minimum wage changes or other legislated labor changes actually been endogenous responses to economic conditions versus some kind of one-time exogenous factors?	13	1
201507	2269	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	But I do think it’s a really interesting question to think about.	13	1
201507	2270	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	To sum up, the steady improvements in the labor market mean we’re closing in on full employment.	13	1
201507	2271	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In fact, in my forecast we reach a natural rate of unemployment this year and fall below it for much of 2016.	13	1
201507	2272	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	By contrast, the progress in our inflation objective has been less clear, with our preferred measure still running well below target.	13	1
201507	2273	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Still, I expect that we will reach our 2 percent inflation objective by the end of next year.	13	1
201507	2274	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And for those who ask what I mean by that, I just mean the quarterly rate.	13	1
201507	2275	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I don’t mean the four-quarter change.	13	1
201507	2276	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Ah, I’m not as crazy as you thought.	13	1
201507	2277	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	13	1
201507	2278	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Longer-run inflation expectations remain well anchored, and I expect additional firming of price inflation with increased activity and further tightening of the labor market.	13	1
201507	2279	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Moreover, the key factors depressing current inflation—namely, the oil price declines and the dollar appreciation—are transitory.	13	1
201507	2280	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Much of the effect of last year’s drop in oil prices has moved through the pipeline, so to speak, and very recent additional declines in oil prices reflecting optimism about renewed Iranian oil exports should only temporarily depress inflation.	13	1
201507	2281	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	A bigger concern is the dollar appreciation, which has been sizable over the past year and may well continue.	13	1
201507	2282	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The dollar appreciation has a considerable but transitory effect on inflation.	13	1
201507	2283	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The consensus from recent research is that a 10 percent rise in the dollar knocks about ½ percentage point off core inflation in the first year.	13	1
201507	2284	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This effect alone explains much of the shortfall in core inflation that we’ve been seeing of late.	13	1
201507	2285	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Importantly, though, this effect quickly fades.	13	1
201507	2286	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Based on this evidence, I expect the effects of the past dollar appreciation and recent dollar appreciation on inflation rates to largely disappear by next year.	13	1
201507	2287	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Looking ahead, there are concerns that our policy normalization, coupled with continued easy monetary policy abroad, will lead to renewed dollar appreciation, and obviously that’s a risk.	13	1
201507	2288	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	But here I agree with the Tealbook:  Further appreciation in the dollar is likely to be moderate.	13	1
201507	2289	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Our upcoming policy normalization is widely anticipated and should be reflected in markets’ expectations.	13	1
201507	2290	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Adding up all of these considerations, I expect we’ll achieve our 2 percent inflation target in the next few years as underlying inflation moves toward our goal and temporary factors dissipate.	13	1
201507	2291	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Still, more data are needed to say confidently—I have to cheat, everybody else does, and get a little policy discussion into this go-round—that our return to our 2 percent goal will be timely.	13	1
201507	2292	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’ll have more to say on that tomorrow.	13	1
201507	2293	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	13	1

201507	2305	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I continue to see the staff’s unemployment forecast as somewhat out of sync with more mainstream views.	13	1
201507	2306	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I see the staff’s forecast as, at certain points, based on a more stretched interpretation of certain aspects of the data than I think is wise to use as a basis for US monetary policy.	13	1
201507	2307	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Pages 2 and 3 of Tealbook A show that the staff’s forecast path for real GDP tends to be lower than private-sector forecasts, as described by the Blue Chip as of July 10, 2015.	13	1
201507	2308	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Similarly, the staff forecast for unemployment tends to be higher than private- sector forecasts, as described by the Blue Chip.	13	1
201507	2309	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The staff’s forecast of unemployment falls to 5.2 percent by the end of 2016—just 0.1 percentage point lower than today over the next 18 months—and to 5.1 percent by the end of 2017—just 0.2 percentage point lower than today over the next 2½ years.	13	1
201507	2310	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I have said before that I do not see this as a credible forecast, taking into account the declines in unemployment in the recent past.	13	1
201507	2311	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	My view is that unemployment is more likely to continue to fall further, and that this should be part of the baseline case for this Committee.	13	1
201507	2312	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	One piece of support for the idea that unemployment will fall further comes from the historical series given on page 15 of Tealbook A.	13	1
201507	2313	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The unemployment rate fell to below 4 percent in the late 1990s; it fell to the middle of the 4 percent range in the 2000s.	13	1
201507	2314	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	In view of the low interest rate environment that’s anticipated over the next few years, and barring an adverse shock, the historical experience of the most recent two expansions is, I think, the best guide to likely developments in the next few years.	13	1
201507	2315	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Page 20 of Tealbook A contains alternative scenarios for unemployment.	13	1
201507	2316	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	Key assumptions are, one, that the ratio of payroll employment to Current Population Survey measures of employment is rising, and, two, that the labor force participation rate will return to an estimated trend line.	13	1
201507	2317	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do not think these are good baseline assumptions.	13	1
201507	2318	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The ratio of payroll employment to CPS employment has risen recently, but it did not rise during the 2002–07 expansion.	13	1
201507	2319	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	It has moved up but may or may not rise further.	13	1
201507	2320	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The labor force participation trend is on page 29 of Tealbook A.	13	1
201507	2321	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is, indeed, an unusual trend line that has not made any contact with the data over the past five years.	13	1
201507	2322	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	The trend is, in fact, everywhere above the data.	13	1
201507	2323	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This strikes me as a stretched interpretation of the participation data.	13	1
201507	2324	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	A more reasonable interpretation would accept the empirical evidence and fit a trend line to the existing data.	13	1
201507	2325	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	In other words, the trend line would run through the data.	13	1
201507	2326	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	This would likely suggest less future improvement in the labor force participation rate.	13	1

201507	2331	2015-07-29	meeting	BULLARD	An additional consideration in my mind is that weekly initial unemployment insurance claims are at the lowest level in decades and that, indeed, if one adjusts for the size of the labor force, the claims are even lower than in historical experience.	13	1

201507	2345	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201507	2346	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Before I talk about what I prepared for these remarks, I wanted to touch on some of the issues that have been brought up in others’ interventions.	13	1
201507	2347	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There is a lot of discussion about a “transitory first quarter,” and I just wanted to clarify the meaning of that.	13	1
201507	2348	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In growth rate terms, it’s correct that we don’t see the very low growth rate that we saw in the first quarter—of near zero, below zero, or maybe we’ll see the revisions later this week that will push it a little bit above zero—carried forward into the second quarter, third quarter, or fourth quarter.	13	1
201507	2349	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Rather, we see more robust growth in those quarters.	13	1
201507	2350	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But many of the stories with regard to that “transitoriness” are actually stories that you would not expect to show up in the level of GDP.	13	1
201507	2351	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the other hand, the low growth that we saw in the first quarter is actually translating into distinctly lower levels of GDP at the end of the year than at least I had anticipated going into the year.	13	1
201507	2352	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We’re not seeing a 3 percent growth rate overall.	13	1
201507	2353	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We’re not seeing catch-up in the second and third quarters.	13	1
201507	2354	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I think when we use the word “transitory,” the stories we tell for that transitoriness should be stories that actually lead to a permanently lower level of GDP going forward to the end of 2015.	13	1
201507	2355	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The seasonal adjustment story has that flavor, but some of the other stories, such as the port strikes and others, seem much more challenging along those lines.	13	1
201507	2356	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	For the second issue that came up, I was very interested to hear from President George about the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City’s analysis of the SPF, about how some of the decline in the forecast is from losing the inflation nutters—those forecasting the 3 percent and above.	13	1
201507	2357	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Losing those forces in the market is actually a challenge for us.	13	1
201507	2358	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you have folks in the market that are betting on inflation being above 3 percent, that helps keep the real interest rate low and helps stimulate the economy.	13	1
201507	2359	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If those folks are exiting the market—are not betting that inflation is going to be high—that actually serves to drive up the real interest rate and push down stimulus, as long as we’re keeping the nominal rates the same, or the expectations of nominal rates the same.	13	1
201507	2360	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is in line with the idea, which I haven’t voiced publicly yet, that Allan Meltzer was actually one of the big forces for accommodation in the past five years, because the idea that inflation was going to be high in the years ahead was actually a very positive force for this Committee, in light of the fact that we’re not able to drive nominal interest rates lower.	13	1
201507	2361	2015-07-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Was that a beta test of that argument?	13	1
201507	2362	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It was a beta test, yes.	13	1
201507	2363	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	[Laughter]	13	1
201507	2364	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Maybe a gamma test.	13	1
201507	2365	2015-07-29	meeting	EVANS	Well, Allan says people don’t always understand nominal versus real, and this argument demands that people understand nominal versus real.	13	1
201507	2366	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn to a discussion of what I prepared.	13	1
201507	2367	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Madam Chair, I’ll first talk about local economic conditions in the Ninth District, then I’ll talk about international risks, and I’ll close by presenting what I see as a key feature of the inflation outlook.	13	1
201507	2368	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	By some metrics, the Ninth District labor market looks very strong.	13	1
201507	2369	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The unemployment rate has fallen to 4 percent in the Ninth District.	13	1
201507	2370	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Indeed, the four states that are located entirely in the District—Montana, North Dakota, South Dakota, and Minnesota—all have unemployment rates below 4 percent.	13	1
201507	2371	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Yet other metrics suggest the labor market recovery is not yet complete in the Ninth District.	13	1
201507	2372	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thus, on average over the past 12 months, the fraction of people aged 25 to 54 in the Dakotas and Montana who have a job remains more than 2 percentage points below its 2007 level.	13	1
201507	2373	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And the fraction of those aged 16 and over who work part time for economic reasons remains high in Montana and South Dakota.	13	1
201507	2374	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But yet, in Minnesota, the metrics are consistent with a more complete labor market recovery.	13	1
201507	2375	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On average over the past year, the fraction of people aged 25 to 54 who have a job has returned to its 2007 level.	13	1
201507	2376	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The fraction of people over the age of 16 who are working part time but would like a full-time job has fallen back to its 2007 level.	13	1
201507	2377	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So if you look at Minnesota, the full range of labor market metrics—the unemployment rate, the PTER, and the 25-to-54 EPOP—have all come back to 2007 levels.	13	1

201507	2382	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think Minnesota’s experience gives some confidence that a broad range of national labor market metrics can return to pre–Great Recession levels—possibly even better—without generating undue inflationary pressures.	13	1
201507	2383	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s on the Ninth District front.	13	1
201507	2384	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn to international risks, where I see two key risks.	13	1
201507	2385	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One is well captured by the alternative scenarios regarding recent developments in China in which adverse international events cause a deterioration in global demand and in global financial market conditions.	13	1
201507	2386	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The result is that inflation in the United States remains below target for an even more extended period of time than in the baseline, which enhances the risk that longer-term inflation expectations will slide downward further.	13	1
201507	2387	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	One way to mitigate these risks that the alternative scenario does not consider is to delay the date of liftoff beyond the September 2015 meeting.	13	1
201507	2388	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	By delaying the date of liftoff beyond that considered in the alternative scenarios, the Committee would essentially be taking on insurance against these eventualities.	13	1
201507	2389	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The second international risk is associated with our own actions regarding the stance of US monetary policy.	13	1
201507	2390	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There is a risk that interest rate increases, especially in the absence of any obvious inflationary pressures, could lead to rapid changes in financial market conditions abroad that could then feed back domestically.	13	1
201507	2391	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In that case, we could well see large capital outflows from the EMEs and Europe as investors seek to take advantage of higher US interest rates.	13	1
201507	2392	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These outflows could have adverse consequences for the US economy in at least three different ways.	13	1
201507	2393	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	First, they could create global financial instabilities that would be problematic.	13	1
201507	2394	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Second, the dollar could rise rapidly, creating downward pressures on the demand for US goods.	13	1
201507	2395	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And, third, the EMEs could feel forced to tighten their own monetary policies in order to keep domestic inflation under control, which would then push the demand for US goods down further.	13	1
201507	2396	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thus, on the international front, I think there are two risks overall: One is associated with exogenous events, and the other is more endogenous to our own actions.	13	1
201507	2397	2015-07-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Finally, let me turn to an assessment of the national inflation outlook, although, I have to say, not much has changed in this dimension from our previous meeting, Madam Chair.	13	1

201507	2415	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201507	2417	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	With respect to economic growth, I think there are both positives and negatives.	13	1
201507	2418	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Together they add up to perhaps a 2 percent growth rate in the second half of the year, with the risks about balanced around that trajectory.	13	1
201507	2419	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I’d put the outlook for consumer spending and residential investment on the positive side.	13	1
201507	2420	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Household spending should be well supported over the near term by solid job gains and a saving rate that is higher than what one would expect on the basis of the level of net worth relative to income.	13	1

201507	2425	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	The biggest risk in my mind, though, remains the employment trend, which has been very sturdy relative to the pace of economic growth.	13	1
201507	2426	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	If that trend stays intact over the next few months—I’m going to speak on the monetary policy part here—then I think the case for monetary policy liftoff will be quite compelling.	13	1
201507	2427	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	But I am reluctant to count on those chickens just yet.	13	1
201507	2428	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	With respect to inflation, I continue to become less worried that inflation will remain persistently below 2 percent.	13	1
201507	2429	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	This reflects several factors.	13	1
201507	2430	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	First, the trend of core inflation has flattened out despite the damping effect of lower oil prices and a firmer dollar—influences that will likely prove to be transitory.	13	1
201507	2431	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Second, a tighter labor market is likely to translate soon into higher compensation trends.	13	1
201507	2432	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	While I know that the linkage between wages and inflation is very tenuous, I still believe that pressure on resources does matter in terms of affecting the inflation outlook.	13	1
201507	2433	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	And, third, my own reading is that there is some scope for higher inflation from a firmer trajectory for shelter and health-care costs.	13	1
201507	2434	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	As we all know, these components have a significant weight in core inflation measures.	13	1
201507	2435	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, I do have one nitpick with the Tealbook—as I said, I mostly agree with it.	13	1
201507	2436	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	But Tealbook A, after noting on page 19 that “readings on longer-term inflation expectations have changed little over the intermeeting period,” goes on to say that “some of these measures seem to have edged down during the past handful of years.”	13	1
201507	2437	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, that’s not really new information.	13	1
201507	2438	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	These developments, Tealbook A continues, “suggest a downside risk to our maintained assumption that expectations will remain well anchored.”	13	1
201507	2439	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Well, I’m not sure I share this view— and I’m channeling President Mester a bit here, I think—because the size of these changes is not that large.	13	1
201507	2440	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	It’s on the order of 15 to 25 basis points.	13	1
201507	2441	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	And as Loretta pointed out, the University of Michigan measure tends to get bounced around by the trajectory of oil prices.	13	1
201507	2442	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	So some of the decline that you see in the University of Michigan measure may just reflect the oil price trends.	13	1
201507	2443	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Moreover, as President Mester also pointed out, some measures of consumer inflation expectations have moved higher recently.	13	1
201507	2444	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	For example, the Federal Reserve Bank of New York’s Survey of Consumer Expectations has shown a clear uptick in expected inflation at the one-year and three-year time horizons in recent months.	13	1
201507	2445	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	The one-year median expected inflation rate has risen 27 basis points since April, to 3 percent.	13	1
201507	2446	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	And the three-year median expected inflation rate has risen 8 basis points since March, to 3 percent as well.	13	1
201507	2447	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Now, I wouldn’t overemphasize those increases.	13	1
201507	2448	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	They’re not very large, either.	13	1
201507	2449	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	But I think it does push against the notion that there’s compelling evidence that substantial risk to the downside in terms of inflation expectations are emerging.	13	1
201507	2450	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I just don’t think the data really support that conclusion.	13	1
201507	2451	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, I just want to talk a little bit about this issue of how much excess slack there is in the labor market.	13	1
201507	2452	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	The Federal Reserve Bank of New York staff has done a lot of work on the issue of how much hours worked would increase if the other measures of unemployment, like U- 6, were to be normalized with the actual level of the unemployment rate measured by U-3.	13	1
201507	2453	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Ayşegül Şahin, who is sitting over here, has done a lot of work on this.	13	1
201507	2454	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	And when you actually ask yourself the question of how much hours worked would you get if all these other measures normalize, it’s not as much as you might think or hope.	13	1
201507	2455	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	It suggests that maybe the unemployment rate overstates the tightness of the labor market by about ½ percentage point.	13	1
201507	2456	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	In other words, if you could get U-6 down to where you’d expect it to be relative to U-3, you might get about ½ percent of additional hours worked.	13	1
201507	2457	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	So it points in the right direction of saying that you have some extra labor market slack, but it’s not a huge increment of slack, in my opinion.	13	1
201507	2458	2015-07-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201507	2459	2015-07-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1

201507	2461	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201507	2462	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The picture of the economy presented in the July Tealbook is consistent with, but a bit weaker than, that presented in the June Tealbook.	13	1
201507	2463	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Personal consumption expenditures are expected to continue to grow at an annual rate of a little more than 3 percent over the remainder of this year, while residential investment is expected to grow at more than 5 percent—a good rate, albeit lower than predicted six weeks ago.	13	1
201507	2464	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Nonresidential private investment is expected to grow at a rate slightly higher than GDP and more rapidly than expected in the June Tealbook, although very recent declines in the price of oil may reduce investment relative to the current forecast for the second half of the year.	13	1
201507	2465	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Government purchases for the second half of the year are expected to grow very slowly, at a rate well below the rate of growth of GDP.	13	1
201507	2466	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	And net exports are, of course, expected to continue to decline relative to GDP in the second half of this year and to reduce the growth rate of GDP in the second half by about 0.7 percentage point.	13	1
201507	2467	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The staff attributes much of the subdued performance of GDP in the first half of the year to two factors:  first, the decline in net exports, and, second, the effects of the lower price of oil on drilling and mining investment.	13	1
201507	2468	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	These are both related to factors that have also contributed to the lower rate of PCE inflation.	13	1
201507	2469	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Particularly interesting is the effect of lower oil prices on GDP growth, which seems to be negative—at least in the short run—rather than positive, as we had expected when the price of oil began its significant decline.	13	1
201507	2470	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Our fear from six weeks ago that events in Greece and China could produce major disturbances in Europe and globally, respectively, in the short run has been significantly reduced by the agreement on a third stabilization program reached with the Greek government earlier this month and by the actions taken by the Chinese government to stabilize the stock market, although the Shanghai Stock Exchange has yet to be stabilized.	13	1
201507	2471	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	At the June meeting, I was worried that the underlying strongly positive trends in the economy—especially in the labor market, where monthly increases in employment had averaged 260,000 in 2014—could be slowing.	13	1
201507	2472	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	That fear was exacerbated by the March increase in employment of only about 120,000, a figure we knew at the June meeting.	13	1
201507	2473	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The fear was alleviated by the second-quarter monthly rate of increase of employment of 220,000 and the staff’s forecast monthly rate of increase of 210,000 for the second half of the year.	13	1
201507	2474	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Thus, the labor market is expected to continue to perform well in the years ahead, albeit with a participation rate that is not expected to rise through 2017.	13	1
201507	2475	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	This outcome is consistent with the staff’s judgment that the rate of unemployment will continue to decline very slowly.	13	1
201507	2476	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The Tealbook is an excellent document, but it may be a bit too interesting, for it includes so many interesting details.	13	1
201507	2477	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	[Laughter]	13	1
201507	2478	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	We could go back to the longer version.	13	1
201507	2479	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	It includes so many interesting details that there are inevitably some facts or trends in it that point down and some that point up, on one or the other of which we are often inclined to expand.	13	1
201507	2480	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	I have just been doing that.	13	1
201507	2481	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	But we need mainly to look at the overall picture of this economy.	13	1
201507	2482	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The overall picture is of an economy growing slowly, at a rate of about 2 percent or a bit higher, but with a labor market that has worked remarkably well over a long period despite having had to contend with hardly any support from fiscal policy, an appreciation of the dollar that has significantly reduced aggregate demand, and some surprisingly negative influences from the decline in the price of oil.	13	1
201507	2483	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	As of now, the unemployment rate is near most estimates of the longer-run rate of unemployment projected by participants in the SEP, and it is projected to continue declining.	13	1
201507	2484	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	When exactly we will reach the longer-run rate, as defined by SEP participants, is hard to say because the Committee’s views on the natural rate can change and because there are margins of slack remaining that are not adequately captured by the difference between the unemployment rate and the estimated natural rate of unemployment.	13	1
201507	2485	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	I would now like to revisit and repeat some points I made at last month’s meeting.	13	1
201507	2486	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	First, it is worth reemphasizing that the labor market has, for some time, seemed much stronger than the GDP data.	13	1
201507	2487	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Since early last year, the unemployment rate has declined by well over 1 percentage point.	13	1
201507	2488	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The difference in the behavior of output and employment has been puzzling since soon after the start of the recovery in 2009, and it was particularly stark in the first quarters of 2014 and 2015 when GDP declined.	13	1
201507	2489	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The staff generally takes the view that when the employment and GDP data appear inconsistent, the employment data are more likely to be accurate than the output data, which is to say that we should put more weight on the behavior of employment than on the behavior of GDP.	13	1
201507	2490	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Of course, we all await Thursday’s revisions of the national income data with keen interest and hope, and we will have those data when we come to make our decision in September.	13	1
201507	2491	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	I remain reasonably confident that we will see inflation beginning to move back toward our 2 percent target before very long.	13	1
201507	2492	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Core PCE inflation in the second quarter was 1.7 percent, but it is expected to decline to 1.3 percent in the fourth quarter before rising to 1.5 percent over the four quarters of 2016 and 1.7 percent for the four quarters of 2017.	13	1
201507	2493	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The core CPI is expected by the staff to be 2 percent in both 2015 and 2016, to be followed by 2.1 percent in 2017.	13	1
201507	2494	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	The price of oil is now declining but looks about to reach a new range from which it is as likely to move up as to move down.	13	1
201507	2495	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	And the Board staff is forecasting that core import prices will resume increasing early next year, which should help support core inflation in the period ahead.	13	1
201507	2496	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	As the influence of declining oil and import prices begins to wane, we will begin to see the core inflation rate moving up.	13	1
201507	2497	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Those will be the inflation rates relevant to any changes in monetary policy we put in place in the next few months.	13	1
201507	2498	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	I am reassured that inflation expectations have remained reasonably steady despite the very low levels of headline inflation.	13	1
201507	2499	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	But, overall, it is not the inflation indicators today that persuade me that we will see inflation rising over our projection horizon.	13	1
201507	2500	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Rather, it is my conviction that economic slack has been diminishing and continues to diminish, and that the Phillips curve will reassert itself, as the incoming evidence is beginning to suggest.	13	1
201507	2501	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Needless to say, I also have concerns about the outlook.	13	1
201507	2502	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	In particular, consumer spending has been disappointing when we consider how real income has been boosted by lower gasoline prices.	13	1
201507	2503	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Industrial production data has likewise been disappointing, and developments in the Chinese economy have yet to play out.	13	1
201507	2504	2015-07-29	meeting	FISCHER	Yet we need to remind ourselves that all of this is taking place against the background of a labor market that is near full employment and is expected to continue to strengthen.	13	1

201507	2514	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	With respect to proposals to raise the minimum wage and actual minimum wage increases, I think there’s a lot, John, to what you say about past efforts to raise minimum wages.	13	1
201507	2515	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	Having been through a couple of those efforts myself, I found that oftentimes what would happen is that those who favored minimum wage increases would wait until a point at which they could make the argument that a nontrivial portion of people covered by minimum wages were already above that wage.	13	1
201507	2516	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	So the net effect on employers would not actually be that great.	13	1
201507	2517	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	One of the salient differences, I think, between the proposals and the things that have been enacted this time around and the past is, that argument isn’t being made.	13	1
201507	2518	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	On the contrary, employers are saying that, basically, they’d have to raise wages for all of their employees.	13	1
201507	2519	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	That’s because the political origins of this particular set of initiatives come more from this sense that, persistently and chronically, wages have not been rising.	13	1
201507	2520	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	So we can’t be sure about it, but my guess is that there is some difference between the effect that the minimum wage increases would have now and what I think you quite rightly intuit as past patterns.	13	1
201507	2521	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	I have a couple of other things.	13	1
201507	2522	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	First, I don’t know about the rest of you, but I would like to try to encourage the staff to continue to do their own economic forecast rather than to attempt to get themselves close to the median of the Blue Chip.	13	1
201507	2523	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	The whole purpose of what we’re asking them to do is to give us an independent, more or less coherent, picture of what they think is going on in the economy, which then allows us to see whether that’s convincing, and not to try to calibrate it so that the final numbers somehow align well with what others are saying.	13	1

201507	2525	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	There are a bunch of people in the Blue Chip who think unemployment is going to be higher and a bunch of people in the Blue Chip who think unemployment is going to be lower.	13	1
201507	2526	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	It’s interesting that one of the things that, as a whole, the Blue Chip has been consistently wrong about for the past seven years is what’s going to happen to inflation.	13	1
201507	2527	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	So, again, while I agree or disagree with particular components of it, I think that it’s really important that we encourage the staff to take an independent view.	13	1

201507	2529	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	Regarding the labor participation rate trend line, again, I don’t think it would be a good idea for the staff to feel that they had to get their trend line to actually track the data for any relatively short period of time.	13	1
201507	2530	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	I believe the coherent argument that has been made by the staff is that, because of the nature of the crisis and the recession, there were substantial dislocations in labor markets, which are only slowly returning to what people would anticipate to be trend.	13	1
201507	2531	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	They’ve adjusted their trend line for labor participation at least twice since I’ve been here, taking into account not only demographics, but also shifts that they’ve seen in the patterns of behavior of middle-aged and older people leaving the workforce permanently sooner rather than later.	13	1

201507	2534	2015-07-29	meeting	TARULLO	On labor markets, as many people have commented, we’ve had continued improvement across most dimensions with the notable exception, I still think, of wages.	13	1
